    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="3788e408c1044297ab5530efa0c34a1d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '901e9071-033d-468a-b0cf-242b09178b73'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d243178735" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>O3-type layered oxides are promising cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. However, unsatisfying cyclic stability hinders its practical application, mainly resulting from harmful phase transition and irreversible oxygen redox, especially in high-voltage regions. Herein, a co-doped strategy by incorporating Li<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>, and Sb<sup>5+</sup> into the O3-Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials is proposed. Both suppressing the undesired phase transition over 4.1&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and reducing the anisotropic strain are achieved with the novel Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>Sb<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LMCS NFM). Moreover, restricted but highly reversible oxygen redox is observed due to strong attraction from Sb<sup>5+</sup> and special “Li–O-vacancy” and “Mg–O-vacancy” configurations. The strategy brings about excellent high-voltage cyclic stability with a reversible capacity of 130&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention of 85% after 250 cycles at 4.2&nbsp;V, and less thermal runaway risk and moisture sensitivity, increasing the probability of O3-type oxide cathode practical applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302793-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In recent years, the rapid development of electric vehicles, portable electric devices, and energy storage stations has driven huge requirements for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0001_R_d243178719e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - ACS Energy Lett. New J. Chem.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the limited availability and uneven distribution of lithium resources encourage concerns about the lack of raw materials and rapidly increased costs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0002_R_d243178719e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Alloys Compd. Rare Met. Molecules Advanced Energy Materials J. Bioresour. Bioprod. Chem. Eng. J.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To address this issue, Various energy storage devices have been developed over the past decades.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0003_R_d243178719e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rec. Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as a promising complementary technology to LIBs. The development of SIBs has attached great significance due to abundant sodium resources, similar work principle,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0004_R_d243178719e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chin. Phys. B">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and wide-temperature performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0005_R_d243178719e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Three kinds of cathode materials including layered oxides, polyanion compounds, and Prussian white analogs are proposed and studied extensively. Among them, layered oxide, Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i>≤1, TM: transition metals) has advantages in the aspects of theoretical capacity, tap density, and easy manufacture.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0006_R_d243178719e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Layered oxides can be categorized into O-type and P-type due to their different O-stacking orders.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0007_R_d243178719e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Physica B+C">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> O3-type layered cathode O3-Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub> generally possesses high sodium content (<i>x</i> &gt;0.8) and high initial coulombic efficiency, but suffers from poor cyclic stability.</p>
         
         <p>For layered oxide, the skeleton structure is formed by virtue of O–O repulsion from TM-O slab, which is stabilized by the inserted Na<sup>+</sup> due to its shielding effect. During the charging process, Na<sup>+</sup> ions are removed along with increasing O–O repulsion and TM layer gliding. Without a strictly limited charge cut-off voltage, layered structure collapse and oxygen release will happen under the deep desoliation condition. At the same time, the cyclic stability fades rapidly at higher voltage, even though accompanied by increased initial capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The common layered oxide, NaNi<i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<i><sub>y</sub></i>Mn<i><sub>z</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> (1&nbsp;&gt;&nbsp;<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, <i>z</i>&nbsp;&gt;&nbsp;0, <i>x</i>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>y</i>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>z</i> = 1), is a promising cathode material for SIBs, owing to its high energy density and low cost. However, its cyclic stability is hindered by the high charging cut-off voltage of 4.2V. Specifically, a harmful phase transition along with severe <i>c</i>-axis shrink and irreversible oxygen redox result in an unstable layered framework and serious capacity fading. Hu et&nbsp;al. reported O3-NaNi<sub>0.65</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.15</sub>O<sub>2</sub> going through an O3→O’3→P3→O3’’ phase transition pathway during the charging process from 2.0 to 4.2&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0008_R_d243178719e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The OP2 phase is observed directly by Ceder et&nbsp;al. after the NaTi<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Co<sub>0.25</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is charged to 4.2&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0009_R_d243178719e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, oxygen oxidation is very likely to happen at high voltage, resulting in TM layer gliding and oxygen release, further deteriorating the reversibility of O3-layered cathode materials. Liu et&nbsp;al. have observed the oxygen oxidation reaction at 4.17&nbsp;V for the NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0010_R_d243178719e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Actually, tough challenges hindering O3-type oxide stable cycling at high voltage include some other secondary difficulties. The lattice mismatch in the harmful phase transition always leads to TM dissolution, especially notorious Mn cations, which will work as a catalysis center for the redox reactions between the released oxygen species and reductive electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0011_R_d243178719e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the Fe-based oxides at high-voltage state, the Fe cation will migrate from TM layer to Na layer because of the strong Jahn–Teller (J–T) distortion effect of octahedral Fe<sup>4+</sup>–O<sub>6</sub> with cation vacancies left, enabling the “vacancy-O-vacancy” configurations to trigger oxygen redox. Unfortunately, the migration is partially reversible and the reactive oxygen species easily escape from the matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0012_R_d243178719e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Batteries Supercaps">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The above difficulties comprehensively lead to the rapid degradation of high-voltage layered oxides.</p>
         
         <p>Many efforts have been devoted to improving the cyclic stability of O3-type layered oxide materials. Note that element doping is an efficient way to stabilize the layered structure at elevated voltages. Mg<sup>2+</sup> was introduced to P-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> to trigger reversible oxygen redox via generated “Mg–O-vacancy” configuration and suppress cation migration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0013_R_d243178719e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar to Na<sup>+</sup> in terms of ion radius, Ca<sup>2+</sup> in Na layer can serve as a “pillar” to stabilize the layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0014_R_d243178719e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Guo et&nbsp;al. reported inactive Li<sup>+</sup> substituted Ni<sup>2+</sup>, and exhibited suppressed J–T effect and reversible phase transition from 2.0 to 4.5&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0015_R_d243178719e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High-valence ion doping, such as Sb<sup>5+</sup>, enables reduced destructive phase transition and higher average voltage due to intense electrostatic repulsion between Sb<sup>5+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0016_R_d243178719e641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater. Electrochim. Acta Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the long-term cycling performance is still unsatisfying because of the limited framework stabilization effect of single-element doping.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we demonstrate a Li–Mg–Ca–Sb co-doping strategy to modify Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> to improve the high-voltage cyclic stability by regulating phase transition and oxygen redox. The resulting layered cathode of Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>Sb<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LMCS NFM) is prepared via a simple high-temperature solid-state method. Integrating the dopants with layered oxide brings about a highly reversible phase transition with alleviated <i>c</i>-axis shrink at high voltage region and solid-solution-like behavior. We use synchrotron soft X-ray spectroscopy (sXAS) and density functional theory (DFT) to verify the suppressed but highly reversible oxygen redox upon cycling with suppressed lattice oxygen release. Moreover, TM migration to Na layers and interface side reactions under deep desodiation condition are also inhibited, leading to stable layered structure and fast ion transportation. Eventually, LMCS NFM exhibits a superior capacity retention of 85% after 250 cycles with much enhanced thermal and moisture stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302793-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) and Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>Sb<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LMCS NFM) were prepared via a high-temperature solid-state method. The experimental procedure of LMCS NFM synthesis is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The overall composition analysis was conducted by inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), and the atoms’ molar ratio is agreeable with the designed stoichiometry. The crystal structures of the as-synthesized materials were tested by X-ray diffraction (XRD). The XRD patterns of NFM and LMCS NFM with corresponding Rietveld refinement are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0001">1a,b</a>. Both materials show high crystallinity with an O3 structure and a space group of R-3m. The superlattice generation can be observed due to quite a size difference between Li<sup>+</sup> dopants and TM ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0017_R_d243178719e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, the impurity of NiO vanishes after doping. The refinement unit cell parameters are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The <i>c</i>-axis is shortened perhaps attributing to Mg<sup>2+</sup> possessing stronger electronegativity compared with Na<sup>+</sup>, thus displaying more extensive attraction with adjacent O<sup>2−</sup>. The surface morphology was characterized by scanning electronic microscopy (SEM). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0001">1c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a,b</a> (Supporting Information), both NFM and LMCS NFM exhibit similar plate-like morphology with a diameter of 1–4&nbsp;µm. The surficial morphology of NFM looks rough, while the particle surface is clean after introducing dopants. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0001">1d</a> shows the transmission electronic microscopy (TEM) image and selected area electronic diffraction (SAED) pattern along the [0 1 0] zone axis. Regular diffraction spots can be well indexed to different crystal planes of the O3 structure as marked. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0001">1e</a> shows the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of LMCS NFM. The lattice stripes can be observed clearly, and the <i>d</i>-spacing is 0.239&nbsp;nm, consistent with the (0 1 2) crystal plane of the O3 structure. The embedded fast Fourier transform (FFT) pattern of the HRTEM image further confirms the hexagonal layered structure of the as-prepared LMCS NFM. Based on the above analysis, the crystal structure of LMCS NFM is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0001">1f</a>. Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Li<sup>+</sup>, and Mg<sup>2+</sup> cations are located on the Na site, while Sb<sup>5+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and Li<sup>+</sup> occupy the transition metal site due to similar ionic radius and valance. Energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0001">1g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) implies the uniform distribution of metal elements, Fe, Sb, Mg, Ca, Mn, and Ni.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302793-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/48e4be75-0dfb-413e-92fa-78e238b9c0c4/aenm202302793-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/48e4be75-0dfb-413e-92fa-78e238b9c0c4/aenm202302793-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/17e0ea84-471c-4c22-b31b-d5b2f2bfc3cf/aenm202302793-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/48e4be75-0dfb-413e-92fa-78e238b9c0c4/aenm202302793-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302793-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterization. Rietveld refinement of XRD for a) LMCS NFM and b) NFM. c) The SEM image, d) SAED patterns, e) the HRTEM image, f) the schematic of crystal structure, and g) the EDS mapping of the LMCS NFM.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202302793-sec-0030-title">2.1 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of NFM and LMCS NFM was evaluated in half cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0002">2a,b</a> shows the selected charge and discharge profiles of LMCS NFM and the NFM at 1 C in 250 cycles (1 C = 140&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. It can be seen that LMCS NFM shows lower capacity than NFM at the very beginning, but the capacity fading of LMCS NFM is much improved. In addition, there is almost no voltage delay during 250 cycles, indicating a highly reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction process. On the contrary, the overpotential continues to increase and the discharge plateau keeps degrading with repeated charge and discharge for NFM. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0002">2c</a>, the discharge medium voltage drop is only 3.06% for the LMCS NFM. However, a huge discharge medium voltage drop of 21.03% is observed in the NFM battery system, perhaps owing to irreversible oxygen redox, cation migration, spinel phase, or even rock salt formation. Interestingly, the voltage plateau at 4.1–4.2&nbsp;V for NFM in the first charge process disappears after multi-element doping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting the phase transition in NFM at 4.1–4.2&nbsp;V may be suppressed in LMCS NFM. The LCMS NFM shows a high capacity retention of 85% after 250 cycles at 1 C rate, and the coulombic efficiency is still 99.85% at the 250th cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0002">2d</a>). In comparison, there is only 32.7% capacity left for the NFM after 250 cycles. The coulombic efficiency is only 98.21% at the 250th cycle. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0002">2e</a> shows the rate capability of LMCS NFM. At the current density of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C, the LMCS NFM displays 130, 121, 110, 102, and 92 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The discharge capacity at 5 C is 71.9% of the initial capacity, slightly lower than that of 72.8% for the NFM, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), which can be explained by the narrow Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path in XRD refinement results. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) results and the calculated ion diffusion coefficient are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). The half cell was charged and discharged at 0.1 C for 20 min with 2 h of relaxation followed. Such a high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> S<sup>−1</sup> confirms excellent rate capability for LMCS NFM.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302793-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cae4dace-c247-409f-ac00-79e670344bb4/aenm202302793-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cae4dace-c247-409f-ac00-79e670344bb4/aenm202302793-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5c68f40e-12c5-4bf4-b3a8-3887980992a8/aenm202302793-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cae4dace-c247-409f-ac00-79e670344bb4/aenm202302793-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302793-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance. The charge and discharge profiles of the a) LMCS NFM and b) the NFM. c) The discharge medium voltage of the NFM and LMCS NFM. d) Cycling performance with the voltage range of 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V of the NFM and LMCS NFM. e) The rate capability of the LMCS NFM. The CV curves for the first five cycles of f) the LMCS NFM and g) the NFM. h) Comparison of this work and the previous research.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The first five cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of NFM and LMCS NFM are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0002">2f,g</a>. The much higher cathodic peak current at 3.0&nbsp;V represents more stored capacity at the lower voltage for LMCS NFM (The mass loading is similar). By contrast, the higher voltage region, especially over 4.0&nbsp;V for the NFM contributes quite a lot of capacity, which agrees with the voltage profiles in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). Storing the capacity at the lower voltage (O3 phase) upon charge is beneficial to cyclic stability according to the previous research.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0018_R_d243178719e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intrinsically unstable phase for NFM at 4.2&nbsp;V, is always associated with oxygen anion oxidation and lattice oxygen loss, resulting in rapid capacity fading. For the CV curves of NFM, the cathodic peak current at ≈4.15&nbsp;V continues to decrease, suggesting poor reversibility of electrochemical reaction at high voltage for NFM. The peaks at the same voltage region overlap well for LMCS NFM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a,b</a>, Supporting Information). If we increase the charging cut-off voltage to 4.5&nbsp;V, the CV curves are recorded in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a> (Supporting Information). The cathodic peak at 4.3&nbsp;V for the first cycle disappears in the following cycles, indicating an irreversible reaction associated with oxygen redox. The faster-declined peak current at 4.15&nbsp;V demonstrates much more fading of this electrochemical reaction compared with Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0002">2g</a>. For LMCS NFM, the well-overlapped curves reveal an excellent stabilized lattice framework even charged to 4.5&nbsp;V. Overall, LMCS NFM demonstrates great advantages in cyclic stability among cathode materials reported recently (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0002">2h</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0014_R_d243178719e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0016_R_d243178719e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater. Electrochim. Acta Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0017_R_d243178719e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0019_R_d243178719e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Mater. J. Colloid Interface Sci. J. Power Sources Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High capacity retention at such an elevated cut-off voltage provides a feasible approach to further improve SIBs energy density.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202302793-sec-0040-title">2.2 Structural and Phase Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>In order to reveal the phase transition behavior of the two cathodes during the first Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process, in-situ XRD was performed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), and then the (003) and (104) peaks were picked for demonstration and shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0003">3</a>. The two cathodes go through a similar phase transition pathway: O3→O3+P3→P3→OP2→P3→O3+P3→O3, which has no O-type structure appearing at the high voltage of 4.2&nbsp;V as the (104) peak indicates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0008_R_d243178719e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0020_R_d243178719e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Power Sources">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the charging voltage reaches 2.94&nbsp;V, corresponding to ≈50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of capacity, a P-type phase appears for NFM. In comparison, the voltage of generating the P3 phase is 3.1&nbsp;V, which is equivalent to the capacity of 74 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The delay of O–P phase evolution demonstrates that the initial phase (O3) is stabilized and maintained well by the foreign dopants even at the very beginning of the initial charging process. Generally, the O–P phase evolution is always associated with <i>c</i>-axis expansion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0003">3b</a>). The phase-transition behavior procrastination means slowing down <i>c</i>-axis expansion during the charging process, which can facilitate the structural evolution reversibility toward longer-term cycling stability. As previously reported,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0018_R_d243178719e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the prolonged O-type phase can store more capacity at the lower voltage. The delay of O–P phase transition is beneficial to achieving higher capacity and energy density in a specific voltage range. The conclusion is consistent with the CV result.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302793-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a9ca08f7-8703-4bdb-b647-4ac0fd21a3e5/aenm202302793-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a9ca08f7-8703-4bdb-b647-4ac0fd21a3e5/aenm202302793-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/187bde7f-3fef-4621-8190-82eff390b4cd/aenm202302793-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a9ca08f7-8703-4bdb-b647-4ac0fd21a3e5/aenm202302793-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302793-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ XRD patterns evolution of (0 0 3) and (1 0 4) diffraction peaks, corresponding charge and discharge curves, and the calculated <i>c</i>-axis evolution for a,b) NFM and c,d) LMCS NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>When the charging voltage reaches 4.1–4.2&nbsp;V, the (003) peak shifts to a higher diffraction angle, meaning rapid shrink of the <i>c</i>-axis, and a new OP2 phase is formed. Based on the initial O3 phase, the diffraction angle difference for NFM is 0.53&nbsp;°, more than twice as large as that of 0.24&nbsp;° for LMCS NFM. As we all know, the amount of shrink is a vital indicator to evaluate whether the lattice matrix is stable or not during the (de)intercalation process. The much more right shift of the (003) peak is related to rapid <i>c</i>-axis contraction for NFM, which is not favorable for long-term cyclic performance. The diffraction angle of (003) peak reflects cell parameters <i>c</i>, which is calculated by the Bragg equation and displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0003">3b,d</a>. The parameter <i>c</i> change of LMCS NFM is 1.2337&nbsp;Å, less than that of 1.5493&nbsp;Å for NFM. It is worth mentioning that the widest <i>c</i>-axis corresponds to the P3 phase and the narrowest <i>c</i>-axis is related to the OP2 phase. The less contraction of <i>c</i>-axis at 4.2&nbsp;V implies stable interlayer spacing. Furthermore, compared with the bare one, the phase evolution without any breakpoint for LMCS NFM makes the Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process look like a solid-solution reaction, associated with less lattice strain during charge and discharge processes.</p>
            
            <p>To further evaluate the foreign elements doping effect in layered structure configuration, SEM and TEM were conducted to observe the morphological and microstructural evolution. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, the surface morphology maintains integrity for LMCS NFM after 200 cycles, but several micro-cracks can be observed clearly on the NFM primary particles. This dissimilarity verifies the mitigated internal strain by incorporating foreign dopants, as in situ XRD patterns illustrated. For NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0004">4c</a>), initiated cracking perpendicular to the <i>c</i>-axis can be observed clearly. It might be ascribed to lattice mismatch and material fatigue due to the repeated sharp fluctuation of lattice parameters during cycling, as illustrated by the schematic diagram below. In the HRTEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0004">4d</a>), a damaged surface region can be observed and further recognized as rock salt phase (space group: Fm-3m) via FFT on the outer surface of NFM particles due to interphase side reactions and partially reversible TM migration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0020_R_d243178719e1020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Power Sources">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The interior region still holds its initial layered structure. It can be explained by organic electrolyte reacting with deeply oxidized cations (Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Ni<sup>4+</sup>) and released oxygen species, eventually resulting in surface reconstruction. The direct redox reactions can be accelerated under the catalysis effect of the dissolved metal elements, especially Mn ions, and the generated micro-cracks significantly increased the contact area (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Eventually, surface reconstruction with the formation of rock salt phase (FeO and NiO) and cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) film appear on the outer surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0021_R_d243178719e1033" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, we speculate that the “Fe-rich” surface due to Mn dissolution contributes to the Fe migration and formation of the rock salt phase under deep desodiation conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0011_R_d243178719e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0022_R_d243178719e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The outer surface rock salt phase always blocks Na<sup>+</sup> transportation due to its poor ion conductivity. It is also one of the reasons for increased polarization and voltage hysteresis for NFM as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0002">2e</a>. Interestingly, there are only clearly visible lattice stripes, verified as (006) lattice plane with the space group of R-3m, both at the near-surface and in the bulk area as described in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0004">4e</a>. It suggests that the original layered structure is maintained well for LMCS NFM. We performed GITT to evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient before and after cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). For the NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0004">4f</a>), the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient significantly drops after cycling, attributed to the rock salt phase and CEI film formation. On the contrary, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient almost has no change for LMCS NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0004">4g</a>), suggesting rock salt phase formation is suppressed, agreeing well with the above TEM images and discharge medium voltage results. The abovementioned results indicate that the foreign dopants keep the layered structure integrity without surface reconstruction even after long-term cycling.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302793-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ce6a3a1b-b011-4b30-b248-9bb1394a3e7e/aenm202302793-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ce6a3a1b-b011-4b30-b248-9bb1394a3e7e/aenm202302793-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/73329bb0-f536-424b-b855-18a98079581c/aenm202302793-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ce6a3a1b-b011-4b30-b248-9bb1394a3e7e/aenm202302793-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302793-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of a) NFM and b) LMCS NFM after 200 cycles. c) The TEM image of NFM and schematic material fatigue. The HRTEM images and FFT (inset) patterns of d) NFM and e) LMCS NFM. The rock salt phase is marked by red rectangle. Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient calculated via GITT for the first cycle and after cycling of f) NFM and g) LMCS NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202302793-sec-0050-title">2.3 Oxygen Stability and Applicable Prospects</h3>
            
            <p>Oxygen anion irreversible oxidation is a persistent challenge for O3 layered oxide at high voltages. Soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS) of O K-edge offers direct evidence for the electronic structure of oxygen. The sXAS in total electron yield (TEY) mode reflecting the surface information within 2–5&nbsp;nm is applied in this research (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0005">5a,b</a>). The peaks before 535&nbsp;eV correspond to the transition of O 1s electron to TM 3d-O 2p hybrid orbitals, and the peaks after 535&nbsp;eV reflect the information of TM 4sp-O 2p hybrid orbitals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0023_R_d243178719e1105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Chem.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The two peaks near 530 and 533&nbsp;eV are associated with <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub> and <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> orbitals, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0024_R_d243178719e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Small">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks near 533&nbsp;eV shift to lower energy when charging to 4.2&nbsp;V, related to TM oxidation. The peak at 530&nbsp;eV appears when charging to 4.2&nbsp;V, meaning the oxidation of oxygen anion. However, the peak of NFM is almost negligible after 100 cycles, attributed to poor oxygen redox stability. The same peak has no change for LMCS NFM, implying highly reversible oxygen redox. The reason is probably that the introduction of Li<sup>+</sup> and Mg<sup>2+</sup> brings about new configurations “Li–O-vacancy” and “Mg–O-vacancy” to activate highly reversible oxygen redox. The integrated pre-edge area offers information about the unoccupied hole states of oxygen 2p orbitals (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0005">5c</a>). The almost unchanged area for the first and 100th charge states indicates highly reversible oxygen redox for the LMCS NFM during the charge and discharge processes. The smaller integrated area of LMCS NFM for the first cycle, compared with the bare one, may be caused by the strong attraction of Sb<sup>5+</sup> to oxygen nonbonding electrons, which implies that the LMCS NFM brings about a suppressed but highly reversible oxygen redox reaction.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302793-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3d554f95-5ce9-496a-bfe4-c9ca73b488a8/aenm202302793-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3d554f95-5ce9-496a-bfe4-c9ca73b488a8/aenm202302793-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e424b21a-b441-4d04-8fc6-9e6ed5278d38/aenm202302793-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3d554f95-5ce9-496a-bfe4-c9ca73b488a8/aenm202302793-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302793-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The oxygen K-edge sXAS under TEY mode at different states for a) the NFM and b) the LMCS NFM. c) The relative integrated pre-edge area of the shadow at the charge state for the first cycle and after 100 cycles. d) The DOS of O 2p for the LMCS NFM and the NFM. The ELF plot of oxygen along (−1 1 1) plane for e) the NFM and f) the LMCS NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Density functional theory (DFT) is used to elaborate the mechanism of the dopants stabilizing the oxygen anion. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0005">5d</a> exhibits the O 2p density of states (DOS) of two materials. According to previous research,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0025_R_d243178719e1157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Small">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the higher DOS of the element beneath the Fermi level (HOMO orbitals), the more easily it is oxidized. In comparison to NFM, there is lower HOMO orbitals energy for the LMCS NFM, implying lower lattice oxygen reactive activity. It indicates the dopants suppress the oxygen redox of NFM, thus decreasing the lattice oxygen gliding and release, agreeing well with the in situ XRD and sXAS results. In addition, the bandgap of LMCS NFM is narrower than that of the NFM, as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), indicating enhanced electronic conductivity. We also calculated the electron localization function (ELF) of oxygen along (−1 1 1) crystal plane for the NFM and LMCS NFM to analyze the free electron distribution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0005">5e,f</a>). In the ELF plot, the blue region represents lower electron density, and the red region means a higher one.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0026_R_d243178719e1169" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It can be seen that the oxygen electron density of NFM is significantly higher than that of LMCS NFM, perhaps due to the strong electrostatic attraction of localized electrons near oxygen from high-valence Sb<sup>5+</sup>. The reduction of lattice oxygen free electron density means weakened ionic bonds and strengthened covalent bonds for TM─O bonds, beneficial to suppressing irreversible oxygen redox. In order to further explore the gas production, differential electrochemical mass spectroscopy (DEMS) is performed and the results are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). It can be seen that no oxygen is detected for the two cathode materials even at high voltage region, probably due to the released oxygen reacting with reductive carbonate electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0023_R_d243178719e1179" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Chem.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Eventually, carbon dioxide escapes into the Ar flow and is detected. The much more released carbon dioxide for NFM represents more oxygen gas escaping from the layered structure under the deep desodiation condition, corresponding to poor reversibility of oxygen redox reaction.</p>
            
            <p>The oxygen release is always accompanied by lattice mismatch and TM dissolution. The metal ions on the separator and counter electrodes were collected and measured via ICP-MS. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0006">6a</a>, the dissolved metal ions of NFM are much higher than that of LMCS NFM, especially Mn ions. It is ascribed to Mn redox, Mn<sup>3+</sup> disproportionation, and Mn<sup>2+</sup> dissolution. In general, the dissolved ions will work as catalysis centers to accelerate electrolyte decomposition, resulting in increased impedance, agreeing well with the above GITT results. Additionally, surface composition analysis at discharged state is conducted by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). According to the O 1s spectrum in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a,b</a> (Supporting Information), we can observe TM─O bonds at ≈528&nbsp;eV clearly for LMCS NFM cathode, but nothing for NFM, indicating a thinner CEI film is formed on the electrode surface corresponding to less electrolyte decomposition after these dopants stabilizing the layered matrix. This point can be confirmed by the higher Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> content, which is generated in electrolyte decomposition reaction in the C 1s spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14c,d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302793-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5bf0ee33-72ba-4329-aa80-90527ebd1a52/aenm202302793-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5bf0ee33-72ba-4329-aa80-90527ebd1a52/aenm202302793-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c6140f2f-9dce-4555-af71-a163e610b6f1/aenm202302793-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5bf0ee33-72ba-4329-aa80-90527ebd1a52/aenm202302793-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302793-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) TM dissolution and b) DSC profiles of the NFM and the LMCS NFM. The full cell electrochemical performance. The charge and discharge curves of c) the half cell LMCS NFM and HC, and d) the LMCS//HC full cell. e) The rate and f) cycling performance of the LMCS//HC full cell.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In order to assess the impact of the doping strategy on the thermal runaway of NFM,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0027_R_d243178719e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) tests were performed for NFM and LMCS NFM after charging to 4.2&nbsp;V (mixed with fresh electrolyte). Three exothermic peaks appear with a temperature range of 250&nbsp;°C–300&nbsp;°C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0006">6b</a>. The first exothermic peaks at 286&nbsp;°C for NFM and 302&nbsp;°C for LMCS NFM can be associated with desodiation state cathode reduction and electrolyte oxidation decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0028_R_d243178719e1239" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The thermal generation of LMCS NFM is 2206&nbsp;J&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, much lower than 3354&nbsp;J&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for NFM. The results suggest that the doping strategy can improve the thermal stability of NFM effectively.</p>
            
            <p>Full cells were assembled with hard carbon (HC) as the anode to estimate the practical application feasibility of the LMCS NFM cathode. The voltage profiles of LMCS and HC are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0006">6c</a>. The charge and discharge curve of the full cell with a voltage range of 1.0–4.2&nbsp;V (vs HC) is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0006">6d</a>. The initial discharge capacity is 114.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (based on cathode material mass) and the energy density is 208&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on total active materials mass) at the current density of 0.1&nbsp;C. The rate performance of full cells was assessed and ≈60&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of discharge capacity is still obtained even at 5 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0006">6e</a>). The full cells maintained 74.2% of initial capacity after 100 cycles as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302793-fig-0006">6f</a>, and the discharge platform has no attenuation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). In order to evaluate the moisture stability, the two materials were exposed to air for 7 days. The XRD patterns are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a> (Supporting Information) and the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> H<sub>2</sub>O and reduced (003) peak intensity implies poor moisture stability for the NFM. The much-reduced capacity and vanished discharge platform also confirm the destroyed layered structure of NFM by water. On the contrary, the LMCS NFM exhibits better moisture stability. In a word, the promising performance of full cells shed light on the practical application possibility of LMCS NFM with a high cut-off voltage of 4.2&nbsp;V.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302793-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we prepared a multi-element co-doped cathode material, LMCS NFM via a high-temperature solid-state method. The synthesized material exhibits a standard O3-type layered structure oxide. During the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction processes, the inhibited harmful phase transition, the extended O3 phase, and the solid-solution-like reaction alleviate internal strain and harvest a reversible phase evolution. At the same time, the suppressed but highly reversible oxygen redox is achieved due to the strong attraction from Sb<sup>5+</sup>, and special “Li–O-vacancy” and “Mg–O-vacancy” configurations. The interface side reactions and Fe cation migration are also suppressed, enabling a well-protected layered structure. Eventually, a high capacity retention of 85% after 250 cycles at such a high voltage of 4.2&nbsp;V and improved thermal and moisture stability offer more possibilities for practical application. Therefore, specific element doping into the layered oxides framework to construct a solid skeleton suppressing harmful phase transition and irreversible oxygen redox provides a feasible approach to high-voltage sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302793-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0080">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0090">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302793-sec-0100-title"> Materials Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>A high-temperature solid-state method was used to synthesize the layered Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>Sb<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials. Stoichiometric amounts of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin, 99%), NiO (Aladdin, 99%), Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin, 99%), MnO<sub>2</sub> (Aladdin, 98%), Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin, 99.5%), MgO (Aladdin, 99%), CaCO<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin, 99.5%), and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (Aladdin, 99%) were ground thoroughly in an agate mortar with absolute ethanol for 2 h. The mixture was dried at 80&nbsp;°C for 12 h and then calcined at 950&nbsp;°C for 10 h at a rate of 5&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> in a Muffle furnace. After cooling down to 150&nbsp;°C, the black powders were transferred into a glovebox filled with argon (Ar) atmosphere.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302793-sec-0110-title"> Electrochemical Tests and Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The cathode, made of active materials, carbon black, and polyvinylidene difluoride (PVDF) with a weight ratio of 8:1:1 in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) as a solvent, was prepared in a dry room. The anode, including hard carbon (HC), carbon black, and sodium alginate (SA) with a weight ratio of 90:5:5. Al foil was used as the current collector for both the cathode and anode. The mass loading was controlled as 3–4&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> for the cathode and 1.5–2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> for the anode. The half cells consisting of a working electrode, Na metal as the counter electrode and reference electrode, and glass fiber membrane as the separator, were assembled in the glovebox with &lt;0.1&nbsp;ppm oxygen and water. The electrolyte was made of EC and PC as the solvent, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> as salt, and 5% FEC as an additive.</p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The cycling and performance were evaluated on the LAND CT2001A and Neware battery test systems. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were conducted on Autolab potentiostat (PGSTAT302N) at a scanning rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was conducted under the condition of 0.1 C charging for 20&nbsp;min and open-circuit relaxation for 2&nbsp;h. The CV and GITT tests were measured in a two-electrode system. Assuming Na<sup>+</sup> transportation in the cathode materials obeys Fick's second law, and molar volume remains constant in the charge and discharge process, the apparent chemical diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) could be calculated as:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202302793-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>In this equitation, <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> is the mass of the active materials, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> is the molar volume, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> is the molecular weight, <i>S</i> is the contact area between electrode and electrolyte, and Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> are the steady-state voltage difference for the rest and total transient voltage difference after charging for time <i>τ</i>, respectively, <i>τ</i> refers to charging or discharging time.</div>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302793-sec-0120-title"> Physical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The crystal of the prepared samples was analyzed by X-ray diffractor (XRD, Bruker D8 Advance). SEM (Hitachi, S4800) and TEM (Tecnai G2 F20 S-Twin) were used to observe the samples’ morphology. The dissolved transition metal ions and sample composition analysis were conducted via inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS, Agilent 7800). Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) was measured by DSC 214 Polyma (Netzsch) at a temperature scan rate of 5&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> with Ar gas flow. The cathode materials were charged to 4.2&nbsp;V and removed from the current collector in the glovebox. And then, they were mixed with fresh electrolyte and sealed in a stainless-steel pan for thermal analysis. The surface analysis of electrodes was performed by X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy (XPS) (KRATOS, Axis Ultra DLD, Al K<i>α</i>). The differential electrochemical mass spectroscopy (DEMS, Shanghai Linglu. Co. Ltd) was conducted with Ar as flow gas at a rate of 1&nbsp;mL&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302793-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302793-sec-0130-title"> Computational Method and Models</h4>
                  
                  <p>All calculations were carried out by using the projector augmented wave method in the framework of the density functional theory (DFT),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0029_R_d243178719e1514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Phys. Rev.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as implemented in the Vienna ab initio Simulation Package (VASP). The generalized gradient approximation (GGA) and Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) exchange functional<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0029_R_d243178719e1519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Phys. Rev.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was used. The plane-wave energy cut-off was set to 500&nbsp;eV, and the Monkhorst–Pack method<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0030_R_d243178719e1524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Phys. Rev. B">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was employed for the Brillouin zone sampling. The convergence criteria of energy and force calculations were set to 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV&nbsp;atom<sup>−1</sup> and 0.01&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Geometry optimizations and electronic structures were calculated by using the spin-polarized GGA plus Hubbard correction U (GGA+U) method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0031_R_d243178719e1536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Phys. Rev. B">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hubbard U parameters of Fe, Mn, and Ni elements were 5.3, 3.9, and 6.2&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0032_R_d243178719e1542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>Sb<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> structure was built by enumerating all Li–Mg–Ca–Sb–Ni–Mn–Fe cation configurations in the transition metal layer of O3-NaCoO<sub>2</sub> primitive cell using the enumeration method<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0033_R_d243178719e1569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Phys. Rev. B">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as implemented in the Pymatgen code.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0034_R_d243178719e1574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Li–Mg–Ca–Sb–Ni–Mn–Fe cation configuration of the most stable NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>Sb<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> structure was determined by ranking all cation configurations by their DFT energies. Moreover, the desoliation structures of Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Ca<sub>0.05</sub>Sb<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with Na-vacancy orderings were also enumerated by the Pymatgen code. The atomic charges were calculated from the charge density grid using the Bader charge analysis code.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302793-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302793-bib-0035_R_d243178719e1616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Chem. Phys.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
